Tark al-Haram: Two Meanings and Rewardability
Definition and classification of tark al-haram

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two meanings of tark al-haram and apply the Hanafi usul rule that reward is only for the wajib act of kaf al-nafs.

Core Idea
Mere non-commission of sin is not rewarded; only active self-restraint (kaf al-nafs) is a wajib act deserving reward.

This lesson explores a subtle but critical distinction in Hanafi usul: whether simply avoiding a forbidden act earns reward. The text clarifies that 'abandoning the forbidden' has two meanings—mere non-action and active self-restraint—and only the latter, as an obligatory act, carries reward. This precision affects how we understand obligations and their recompense.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two meanings
Read the passage to find the two distinct meanings of 'tark al-haram': (1) adam al-fi'l (mere non-commission) and (2) kaf al-nafs (active self-restraint).
The author explicitly states these two categories.
2
Apply the Hanafi principle on reward
Recall that in Hanafi usul, reward is only for acts (af'al), not for absences of action. Thus, mere non-commission is not an act and not rewarded.
This principle is implicit: 'ثواب فعل واجب پر ہوتا ہے'.
3
Classify kaf al-nafs as wajib
Notice that when temptation and ability are present, restraining oneself becomes a commanded action (wajib). Therefore, kaf al-nafs falls under the category of wajib acts and earns reward.
The text says: 'اس وقت اپنے آپ کو معصیت و حرام سے روکنا واجب ہے'.
4
Examine the Quranic proof
The passage references a Quranic verse (likely 'فَاتَّقُوا اللَّهَ مَا اسْتَطَعْتُمْ') as evidence. Recognize that the command to 'fear Allah' is interpreted as active restraint, not mere avoidance.
The text states that the same answer applies: the verse commands kaf al-nafs.
5
Synthesize the final classification
Conclude that 'tark al-haram' as adam al-fi'l is neither rewarded nor punished (neutral), while as kaf al-nafs is rewarded. The author's original statement is correct when understood in the first sense.
The summary reiterates this distinction.

Source Text

مسک الملیح شرح التوضيح والتطويح) (۲) اگر ترک حرام بمعنی کف النفس ہو تو بلاشبہ اس پر ثواب ہوتا ہے کیونکہ یہ عمل واجب ہے کیونکہ جب اسباب جاذ بہ الی الحرام غلط ہے اس لیے ترک حرام بمعنی عدم النفعل لا یشاب میں داخل ہے اس پر کوئی ثواب نہیں ہوتا کیونکہ ثواب فعل واجب پر ہوتا ہے موجود و مہیا ہوں اور نفس کا میلان الی الحرام ہو اس وقت اپنے آپ کو معصیت و حرام سے روکنا واجب ہے تو ترک حرام بمعنی كف النفس واجب میں داخل ہے لہذا اس پر ثواب ہوگا تو معلوم ہوا کہ مثاب علیہ ( جس پر ثواب حاصل ہو ) فعل واجب ہے نه که ترک حرام اور دلیل دوم قرآن پاک کی آیت کا بھی یہی جواب ہے کہ وہاں بھی تھی نفس سے مراد کف النفس عن المحرمات ہے اور اس میں کسی کو نزاع نہیں ہے کہ ترک حرام بمعنی کف النفس بیثاب علیہ میں داخل ہے خلاصہ یہ ہے کہ قول مصنف ترک حرام نہ لا یشاب میں درج ہے اور نہ لا یعاقب میں درست ہے کیونکہ یہاں ترک حرام بمعنی عدم فعل الحرام و عدم مباشره الحرام ہے اور بائیں معنی ترک حرام نہ الائی اب میں داخل ہے اور نہ ہی لا یعاقب میں فتفکر
Misk al-Malih Sharh al-Tawdih wa al-Talwih. (2) If abandoning the forbidden means restraining the soul (kaf al-nafs), then undoubtedly there is reward for it, because it is an obligatory act. Because when the causes attracting towards the forbidden are present and the soul inclines towards the forbidden, at that time restraining oneself from sin and the forbidden is wajib. So abandoning the forbidden in the sense of kaf al-nafs is included in wajib, hence there will be reward for it. So it is understood that that which is rewarded is the wajib act, not the abandonment of the forbidden. And the second proof from a Quranic verse also receives the same answer: that there too the phrase 'fear' means kaf al-nafs 'an al-muharramat, and there is no dispute that abandoning the forbidden in the sense of kaf al-nafs is included in that which is rewarded. The summary is that the author's statement that 'abandoning the forbidden is neither included in that which is rewarded nor in that which is not punished' is correct, because here abandoning the forbidden means non-commission of the forbidden and non-engagement with the forbidden, and in that sense abandoning the forbidden is included neither in that which is rewarded nor in that which is not punished. So reflect.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of the concept of 'tark al-haram' (abandoning the forbidden). The commentator distinguishes two meanings: (1) tark al-haram as 'adam al-fi'l (mere non-commission) and (2) tark al-haram as kaf al-nafs (self-restraint in the face of temptation). The first meaning carries no reward because it is not an act; it is simply the absence of action. Reward is only for acts (af'al), specifically for obligatory acts (wajib). The second meaning is itself a wajib act because when a person has the means and inclination to commit a sin, restraining oneself is a commanded action. Therefore, it is rewarded. The commentator mentions a Quranic verse (likely 'fa-inna ma'a al-'usri yusra' or similar) as proof, but does not quote it, only stating that the command to fear Allah includes this active restraint. The summary confirms that the author's original statement (likely that tark al-haram is neither rewarded nor punished) is correct when tark is taken as mere non-commission. However, if tark is understood as kaf al-nafs, it is rewarded. This precision is critical in Hanafi usul because it determines whether an action is subject to reward and punishment.

Key Terms
ترک حرام (tark al-haram): Abandoning a forbidden act; may refer to mere non-commission (عدم الفعل) or to active self-restraint (کف النفس) which is a wajib act.
کف النفس (kaf al-nafs): Restraining oneself from sin despite inclination and ability; considered a wajib act in Hanafi law.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) The person must have made a verbal intention to abandon the sin.

Key Takeaway
Only tark al-haram as active self-restraint (kaf al-nafs) is rewarded; mere non-commission carries no reward.

Defining Jawāz in the Fourth and Fifth Probabilities
Analyzing the meaning of jawāz in different conceptual probabilities

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two meanings of jawāz (specific possibility and general possibility) and apply the correct meaning in different contexts within Hanafi uṣūl al-fiqh.

Core Idea
Jawāz in Hanafi uṣūl has two distinct meanings: specific possibility (equal sides) and general possibility (non-prohibition of act); the correct meaning depends on the conceptual context.

This passage from a commentary on al-Tawḍīḥ wa al-Talwīḥ tackles a subtle objection: if jawāz means 'both sides equally permitted', then the act of wājib cannot be called permissible. But the matn's classification seems to include wājib under permissible acts. The commentator Taftāzānī resolves this by showing that the meaning of jawāz shifts between the fourth and fifth probabilities. Understanding this refinement is essential for correctly applying the five-fold classification of rulings in Hanafi uṣūl.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two meanings of jawāz
Specific possibility (imkān khāṣṣ): both act and omission are equally allowed. General possibility (imkān ‘āmm): the act is not prohibited.
These two meanings are not interchangeable; they serve different functions in the classification of rulings.
2
Locate the conceptual probabilities
The author of the matn discusses five probabilities. Focus on the fourth (where jawāz contrasts with wujūb) and the fifth (where jawāz includes wujūb).
The probability determines which meaning of jawāz is operative.
3
Analyze the objection
The objector argues: if jawāz means specific possibility in the fourth, then wājib cannot be included; if general possibility in the fifth, then jawāz cannot contrast with wujūb.
This seems to create a contradiction between the two probabilities.
4
Examine Taftāzānī's response
Taftāzānī responds that each probability takes the meaning that fits: in the fourth, jawāz is specific possibility (contrasting with wujūb); in the fifth, it is general possibility (including wujūb, contrasting with ḥurma).
The meaning of jawāz is context-dependent, resolving the apparent contradiction.
5
Apply the response to the matn's statement
The matn's phrase 'the act other than ḥarām and makrūh tahrīmī is permissible' seems to include wājib, but the author explicitly excludes wājib (and the omissions of ḥarām/makrūh tahrīmī) by stating they are obligatory, as quoted in the passage.
This explicit exclusion resolves the objection: wājib is not included in 'yajūzu lahā' under the specific possibility meaning.

اشکال یہ ہوتا ہے کہ جب آپ نے احتمال رابع میں جواز سے امکان خاص مراد لیا ہے تو اب مصنف کی یہ عبارت فعل ماسوی الحرام والمکروہ تحریما وترک ماسوی الواجب مما یجوز لھا صحیح نہیں ہوگی کیونکہ مصنف کے قول کے مطابق فعل حرام اور فعل مکروہ تحریمی کے سوئی باقی فعل کی جتنی اقسام ہیں وہ سب کی سب يجوز لها میں داخل ہوں گی ان قسموں میں سے ایک فعل واجب بھی ہے لہذا افعل واجب بھی يجوز لها میں داخل ہو جائے گا حالانکہ احتمال رابع میں فعل واجب يجوز لھا میں داخل نہیں ہے کیونکہ یہاں جواز کو امکان خاص کے معنی میں لیا گیا تھا یعنی جانب فعل وعدم فعل دونوں مساوی ہوں اور بائیں معنی واجب يجوز لها میں داخل نہیں ہو سکتا کیونکہ اس میں جانب فعل ضروری ہوتی ہے اور جانب ترک ممنوع ہوتی ہے اس طرح مصنف نے یہ فرمایا کہ ترک واجب کے سوئی باقی جتنے تروک ہیں وہ بھی سب کے سب یجوز لها میں داخل ہوں گے یہ بھی صحیح نہیں ہے کیونکہ ان میں ترک حرام بھی اور ترک مکروہ تحریمی بھی داخل ہے حالانکہ یہ دونوں یجوز لها میں داخل نہیں ہیں کیونکہ جواز سے امکان خاص مراد لیا گیا جس کا مطلب عدم منع الفعل والترک ہے ( جانب فعل و ترک دونوں برابر ہوں ) اور یہ معنی ترک حرام اور ترک مکروہ تحریمی میں صحیح نہیں کیونکہ ان دونوں میں جانب فعل و ترک دونوں برابر نہیں ہوتیں ، بلکہ جانب ترک ضروری ہوتی ہے۔ جواب ! The necessity of one side is negated, for example it will be said: jawāz (permissibility) in the sense of non-prohibition of omission, or jawāz in the sense of non-prohibition of the act, i.e., its act is not prohibited. With this meaning, wujūb (obligation) itself enters into jawāz, and is not opposed to it, although in the fourth probability wujūb was in contrast to jawāz and not included in it. The summary of the objection is: if specific possibility is intended, then it is not correct in the fifth probability; if general possibility is intended for jawāz, then it is not correct in the fourth probability. Response: The commentator Taftāzānī responds: Why do you not take the meaning of possibility that is correct in each probability as the intended meaning there? Therefore, in the fourth probability, jawāz will be in the meaning of specific possibility, and jawāz's meaning will be: both the side of action and the side of omission are not prohibited, rather both are optional. Thus with this meaning, it contrasts with wujūb, and wujūb is not included in it, because in wujūb the side of action is necessary. And in the fifth probability, since wujūb is included in jawāz, jawāz will mean general possibility, i.e., jawāz in the sense of non-prohibition of the act, so its act is not prohibited. Now jawāz will come in contrast to hurma (prohibition), because in harām the side of action is prohibited, and wujūb remains included and contained in jawāz. If it is said: when jawāz is intended to mean non-prohibition of act and omission, then his statement 'the act of other than harām and makrūh tahrīmī...' is not correct. The object of objection and response: The objection is: when you in the fourth probability intended jawāz to mean specific possibility, then the author's statement 'the act other than harām and makrūh tahrīmī and the omission other than wājib are among that which is permissible for her' would not be correct, because according to the author's saying, all types of acts except harām and makrūh tahrīmī would be included in 'yajūzu lahā'. Among these types is the act of wājib, so the act of wājib would also enter into 'yajūzu lahā', although in the fourth probability the act of wājib is not included in 'yajūzu lahā', because here jawāz was taken in the meaning of specific possibility, i.e., both sides of action and non-action are equal, and with this meaning wājib cannot enter into 'yajūzu lahā', because in it the side of action is necessary and the side of omission is prohibited. Similarly, the author said that all other omissions except the omission of wājib are also included in 'yajūzu lahā'. This is also not correct, because among them are the omission of harām and the omission of makrūh tahrīmī, although these two are not included in 'yajūzu lahā', because jawāz was intended to mean specific possibility, which means non-prohibition of act and omission (both sides equal). And this meaning is not correct in the omission of harām and the omission of makrūh tahrīmī, because in these two the sides of action and omission are not equal; rather the side of omission is necessary. Response: The summary of the response is: although according to the apparent wording of the author, the act of wājib, the omission of harām, and the omission of makrūh tahrīmī should be included in 'mimmā yajūzu lahā', they are not included, because the author has explicitly stated that these three are among 'mimmā yajibu alayhā', as the author's statement is: 'And the act of the obligatory, and the omission of harām and makrūh tahrīmī are among that which is obligatory for her.' So after the explicit statement, the objection becomes futile, because these three are specifically excluded from 'yajūzu lahā'.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement by the commentator Taftāzānī addressing the meaning of the term jawāz (permissibility) in different conceptual probabilities. The author of the matn (al-Tawḍīḥ) had discussed five probabilities regarding the classification of actions. Here, the fourth and fifth probabilities are analyzed. The objection is that if jawāz is taken as specific possibility (equal sides) in the fourth probability, then wujūb (obligation) is not included; but in the fifth probability, jawāz must be general possibility (non-prohibition of the act) to include wujūb. Taftāzānī responds that each probability takes the meaning that fits. Then a further objection arises: if jawāz in the fourth probability means specific possibility, then the matn's statement 'the act other than harām and makrūh tahrīmī is permissible' would incorrectly include the act of wājib. The answer is that the author explicitly excluded wājib (and also ommission of harām and makrūh tahrīmī) by stating they are obligatory, so they are exceptions. This shows the importance of distinguishing between general and specific meanings of permissibility in Hanafi legal theory.

Key Terms
جواز (jawāz): In this passage, jawāz has two meanings: 1) specific possibility (imkān khāṣṣ) where both act and omission are equally allowed; 2) general possibility (imkān ‘āmm) where only the act is not prohibited, i.e., non-prohibition of the act.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the fourth probability discussed in this passage, what is the meaning of jawāz?
A) General possibility (imkān ‘āmm) – non-prohibition of the act
B) Specific possibility (imkān khāṣṣ) – equal permission of act and omission
C) Obligation (wujūb)
D) Prohibition (ḥurma)

Key Takeaway
Jawāz in Hanafi uṣūl is not a monolithic term; its meaning shifts between 'specific possibility' (equal sides) and 'general possibility' (non-prohibition of act) depending on the conceptual context, which directly affects the classification of acts like wājib.

Inclusion of Makrūh Tahrīmī in Ḥarām
Definitional expansion of ḥarām to include makrūh tahrīmī

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the Hanafi concept of ḥurma (prohibition) and explain why makrūh tahrīmī is included under this category.

Core Idea
In Hanafi usul, 'prohibition' includes both harām and makrūh tahrīmī as acts prohibited by textual evidence, differing only in certainty.

This passage addresses an objection that a classification of permissible and prohibited is incomplete because it omits makrūh tahrīmī. The Hanafi response redefines 'prohibition' broadly to include any act prohibited by textual evidence, whether definitive (harām) or presumptive (makrūh tahrīmī). Understanding this definitional expansion is key to grasping Hanafi legal categories.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Recognize the claim that the author's division of 'what is permissible' and 'what is prohibited' fails because makrūh tahrīmī fits neither.
The objector restricts 'prohibition' to harām only.
2
Unpack the assumption
The objector assumes that ḥurma (prohibition) is limited to acts proven by definitive evidence (dalīl qaṭʿī).
This is a narrow definition.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
The response broadens ḥurma to include any act prohibited by textual evidence, whether definitive or presumptive (dalīl ẓannī).
Thus, makrūh tahrīmī is a type of prohibition.
4
Recognize the usul principle
The Hanafi principle: commands and prohibitions (amr/nahy) are general and apply to all categories unless specified otherwise.
Here 'prohibition' (ḥurma) is taken in its general sense.
5
Apply to the classification
With this broader definition, the author's division covers all categories: permissible and prohibited (including both harām and makrūh tahrīmī).
The objection is resolved.

Source Text

الثالث أن ما يحرم عليها في الوجه الخامس الخ غرض جواب سوال مقدر ۔ سوال ! یہ ہوتا ہے کہ مصنف نے احتمال رابع میں مالها وما عليها ن مايجوز لها وما يحرم عليها مراد لیا ہے اور پھر فرمایا یشملان جميع الاصناف اى الجواز والحرمة، تو مصنف کا یہ قول صحیح نہیں ہے کیونکہ یہ احتمال جمیع اصناف کو شامل نہیں اس لیے کہ مکروہ تحریمی نہ تو یجوز میں داخل ہے وھوالظا ہر اور نہ ہی حرم میں داخل ہے کیونکہ یس حمر مشخص بالحرام ہے اور مکروہ تحریمی صنف آخر حرام کے علاوہ ہے تو یہ حرام میں بھی داخل نہیں ہے جواب ! حاصل جواب یہ ہے کہ قول مصنف صحیح ہے اور مکروہ تحریمی سحرم میں داخل ہے کیونکہ یہاں حرام کا معنی ہے منع عن الفعل خواہ بدلیل قطعی ہو یا بدلیل ظنی لہذا سحرم حرام اور مکروہ تحریمی دونوں کو شامل ہوگا فصح قول فیشملان جميع الاصناف۔
Third: That what is prohibited for her in the fifth way. The object of an implicit objection and response. The objection is: The author in the fourth probability intended by 'what is for her and what is against her' that which is permissible for her and that which is prohibited for her, and then said they encompass all categories, i.e., permissibility and prohibition. So the author's statement is not correct, because this probability does not include all categories, since makrūh tahrīmī is neither included in permissibility – and that is apparent – nor is it included in prohibition, because 'prohibition' is specified for harām, and makrūh tahrīmī is another category besides harām, so it is not included in prohibition either. Response: The summary of the response is that the author's statement is correct, and makrūh tahrīmī is included in 'prohibition' because here 'prohibition' means prohibition of the act, whether by definitive evidence (dalīl qaṭ‘ī) or by presumptive evidence (dalīl ẓannī). Therefore, 'prohibition' will include both harām and makrūh tahrīmī. So the statement 'they encompass all categories' stands correct.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage is an expansion probe clarifying the scope of the term ḥarām (prohibition) in the context of the fifth probability of legal categories. The objection states that the author's claim that his classification of 'what is permissible' and 'what is prohibited' covers all categories fails because makrūh tahrīmī (reprehensible approaching prohibition) falls neither under permissibility nor under prohibition if prohibition is restricted to harām. The response explains that in this context, 'prohibition' (ḥurma) is used in a broader sense to mean any prohibition of the act, whether the evidence is definitive (as in harām) or presumptive (as in makrūh tahrīmī). Thus, makrūh tahrīmī is included under 'what is prohibited', and the classification is comprehensive. This reflects the Hanafi approach of considering makrūh tahrīmī as a mitigated form of prohibition.

Key Terms
حرام (ḥarām): In this passage, ḥarām is defined as any prohibited act, whether by definitive evidence (dalīl qaṭʿī) like the Quran, or by presumptive evidence (dalīl ẓannī) like a solitary hadith. This includes both harām and makrūh tahrīmī.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi response, what is the basis for including makrūh tahrīmī under the category of prohibition?
A) It is explicitly mentioned in the Quran.
B) The definition of prohibition is broadened to include any act prohibited by textual evidence, whether definitive or presumptive.
C) Makrūh tahrīmī is actually a type of permissible act.
D) The author's classification was corrected by adding a new category.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, the term 'prohibition' (ḥurma) is broad, including both harām and makrūh tahrīmī, distinguished only by the strength of evidence.

Taftazani's Objection to the Second Definition of Fiqh
Responding to Taftazani's criticism of the definition transmitted from Shafi'is

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi criteria for evaluating definitions of fiqh and distinguish between the first (Abu Hanifah) and second (Shafi'i) definitions regarding the scope of duties.

Core Idea
A definition of fiqh is valid if its terms are understood by the salaf, even if they admit multiple interpretations—the infinity objection is avoided by limiting duties to those arising in religious life.

In this passage, we examine two objections raised by Taftazani against the second definition of fiqh (attributed to the Shafi'is) and the Hanafi responses. The first objection concerns the ambiguity of the term 'ahkam', which could imply infinite rulings; the second objects to using words with multiple meanings in definitions. The Hanafi defence relies on the early scholars' practice and the contextual limitation of duties to what pertains to the self in religious life. Understanding this dispute clarifies the Hanafi approach to defining foundational terms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Definitions
The first definition is attributed to Imam Abu Hanifah: 'ma'rifat al-nafs ma laha wa ma alayha' (knowledge of what the self owes and is owed). The second definition, transmitted from the Shafi'is, is 'al-'ilm bi al-ahkam' (knowledge of rulings).
These definitions represent different focuses: self vs. rulings.
2
State Taftazani's First Objection
Taftazani argues that the same objection the author raised against the second definition—namely, that 'ahkam' could mean all (infinite) rulings, some, or unspecified—also applies to the first definition's 'ma laha wa ma alayha'.
He claims the first definition is equally ambiguous and thus invalid.
3
Analyse the First Hanafi Response
The Hanafi authors give three responses: (1) out of respect for Imam Abu Hanifah, the author did not mention this objection; (2) the author intended to criticize both definitions; (3) the first definition's 'ma laha wa ma alayha' refers only to duties that arise in one's religious life, which are finite and knowable, unlike the second definition's 'ahkam' which are potentially infinite.
The third response is the most substantive: it resolves the infinity problem by restricting the scope.
4
State Taftazani's Second Objection
Taftazani objects that using a word with multiple probabilities (five in this case) in a definition is ugly (qabih) and not commendable if no qarinah (contextual evidence) specifies one meaning.
He expects definitions to be univocal and precise.
5
Examine the Second Hanafi Response
The Hanafi reply: the early scholars (salaf) did not consider such usage ugly, and this definition is from them. Moreover, the definition is not rendered ambiguous because the five probabilities all pertain to duties, and the intended meaning is clear from the context of religious obligations.
The Hanafis prioritize transmitted usage over later logical strictures.

ثم لا يخفى ان اعتراضه على التعريف الثانى الخ غرض شارح تفتازانی اعتراضین علی المصنف - اعتراض اول یہ کہ مصنف نے تعریف ثانی ( جو کہ شوافع سے منقول ہے) پر ایک اعتراض وارد کیا ہے وہی اعتراض بعینہ تعریف اول پر بھی وارد ہوسکتا ہے یہاں مصنف نے سکوت کیوں اختیار کیا ہے اس اعتراض کو کیوں ذکر نہیں کیا وہ اعتراض یہ ہے کہ ھو العلم بالا حکام میں احکام سے کیا مراد ہے یا کل احکام مراد ہونگے یہ باطل ہے کیونکہ مکمل احکام غیر متناہی ہیں ان کا علم محال ہے یا بعض معین مراد ہونگے یہ بھی باطل ہے کیونکہ کل احکام غیر متناہی ہونے کی وجہ سے مجہول ہیں تو بعض کی تعین نہیں ہو سکے گی یا غیر معین مراد ہونگے وہ بھی مجہول ہیں لہذا یہ تعریف درست نہیں ہے وہی اشکال یہان معین مراد ہیں یا غیر معین وہی اشکالات یہاں بھی وارد ہو نگے جو تعریف ثانی میں وارد ہوئے تھے۔ جواب ! (1) تسلیم ہے کہ یہاں بھی یہ اشکال وارد ہو گا لیکن مصنف نے ادبا یہ اشکال ذکر نہیں کیالان هذ التعريف منقول عن الامام ابی حنیفہ ۔ جواب ! (۲) ثم اعلم سے مصنف نے فقط تعریف ثانی پر اشکال نہیں کیا بلکہ دونوں تعریفوں پر اشکال کرنا چاہتا ہے سعد نے یہ سمجھا کہ اعتراض فقط ثانی تعریف کے ساتھ خاص ہے اس لیے مصنف پر اشکال وارد کر دیا۔ جواب ! (۳) در اصل تعریف ثانی یہ ہے، جو العلم بالا حکام اور احکام سے کل احکام مراد نہیں ہو سکتے کیونکہ یہ غیر متناہی ہیں انسان کی طاقت سے ان کا احاطہ ناممکن ہے لیکن تعریف اول میں معرفتہ النفس مالھا ہے اور یہاں جمیع مالھا و ما علیھا مراد ہو سکتے ہیں کیونکہ مالھا و ما علیھا سے مطلق مالها وما عليها مراد نہیں بلکہ وہ مالھا وما علیھا مراد ہیں جو متعلق بالنفس ہیں اور نفس کو جو اپنی حیوۃ دینو یہ میں مالھا و ما علیھا پیش آتے ہیں وہ ان جمیع کی معرفت حاصل کر سکتا ہے لہذا تعریف ثانی والا اشکال تعریف اول پر وارد نه ہوگا ۔ مع ان اطلاق لفظ اعتراض دوم! مصنف نے فرمایا کہ مالھا و اعلیھا میں پانچ احتمالات ہیں تو اشکال یہ ہے کہ تعریفات میں ایسے الفاظ کا ذکر کرنا جو متعدد معانی کا احتمال رکھتے ہوں قبیح اور غیر مستحسن ہے جب کہ کسی معنی کی تعیین پر کوئی قرینہ نہ ہو یہاں بھی ایسا ہی ہے لہذا یہ تعریف غیر مستحسن ہے۔ جواب! (۱) قدماء کے نزدیک یہ غیر مستحسن نہیں ہے اور یہ تعریف بھی قدماء کی ہے۔ جواب ! (۲) لفظ محتمل للمعانی المتعد وہ کا استعمال في التعریفات اس وقت قبیح اور غیر مستحسن ہے جب کہ ان معانی میں سے فقط ایک ہی مراد وعلی العین لیکن اس معنی معین پر کوئی قرینہ نہ ہو اس صورت میں قباحت لازم آئے گی کیونکہ جہالت فی
Then it is not hidden that his objection to the second definition... Purpose: the commentator Taftazani has two objections against the author. First objection: the author made an objection against the second definition (which is transmitted from the Shafi'is) and that same objection could also be made against the first definition. Why did the author remain silent and not mention this objection? The objection is: in 'huwa al-'ilm bi al-ahkam', what is meant by 'ahkam'? If all rulings are meant, that is invalid because total rulings are infinite, their knowledge is impossible. If some specific rulings are meant, that is also invalid because the total rulings are unknown due to infinity, so specification is not possible. If unspecified rulings are meant, they are also unknown. Hence this definition is not correct. The same objections apply here—whether specified or unspecified—as applied to the second definition. Answer (1): It is conceded that this objection would apply here, but the author, out of politeness, did not mention it because this definition is transmitted from Imam Abu Hanifah. Answer (2): From 'thumma a'lam', the author intended to object to both definitions, not just the second. Sa'd [Taftazani] thought the objection was specific to the second, so he made an objection against the author. Answer (3): In reality, the second definition is 'huwa al-'ilm bi al-ahkam', and 'ahkam' cannot mean all rulings because they are infinite, impossible for humans to encompass. But in the first definition, 'ma'rifat al-nafs ma laha', here all of 'ma laha wa ma alayha' can be meant because 'ma laha wa ma alayha' does not mean absolute [duties] but those duties that pertain to the self, and the self can know all of them in its religious life. Therefore, the objection to the second definition does not apply to the first definition. Second objection: The author said that 'ma laha wa ma alayha' has five probabilities. The objection is that in definitions, using words that admit multiple meanings is ugly and not commendable when there is no evidence to specify one meaning. Here it is like that, so this definition is not commendable. Answer (1): For the early scholars, this is not considered ugly, and this definition is from them. Answer (2): Using a word that admits multiple meanings in definitions is only ugly and not commendable when only one of those meanings is intended but there is no evidence for that specific meaning. In that case ugliness follows because of ignorance.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage is a dispute (usul dispute) regarding definitions of fiqh. Taftazani (the super-commentator) raises two objections against the author (al-Musannif, likely Sadr al-Shariah). First, the author criticized the second definition of fiqh (from the Shafi'is) but his own first definition suffers from the same problem: the term 'ahkam' or 'ma laha wa ma alayha' is ambiguous—does it mean all duties, some, or unspecified? The commentator provides three responses: (1) The author respected Imam Abu Hanifah from whom the first definition is transmitted, so he avoided criticizing it; (2) The author actually meant to criticize both definitions equally, but Taftazani misunderstood; (3) The two definitions are different: in the first, 'ma laha wa ma alayha' refers specifically to those duties that pertain to a person in his religious life, which are finite and knowable, whereas in the second, 'ahkam' is indefinite and could imply infinite rulings. The second objection: using a word with multiple probabilities in a definition is not commendable because it causes ambiguity. The Hanafi response: the early scholars (salaf) did not consider this problematic, and if a word has multiple meanings but the intended meaning is clear from context (as here, from the five probabilities), it is acceptable. This dialogue highlights the Hanafi preference for definitions that are comprehensive yet derived from the transmitted text of Imam Abu Hanifah.

Key Terms
تعريف (ta'rif): A definition that should clearly specify the essence of the defined term
احكام (ahkam): Rulings of fiqh, plural of hukm
قبيح (qabih): Ugly or blameworthy in definition methodology

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator argue that the first definition of fiqh (from Imam Abu Hanifah) does not suffer from the same objection as the second?
A) Because the first definition is transmitted from the Prophet
B) Because 'ma laha wa ma alayha' refers only to duties that arise in one's religious life, which are finite and knowable
C) Because Imam Abu Hanifah explicitly intended all possible rulings
D) Because the objection applies only to Shafi'i definitions

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definition of fiqh is valid because its ambiguity is resolved by the context of religious duties and transmitted precedent from the salaf.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling

Dissecting the Second Definition of Fiqh
Expanding the second definition of fiqh from al-Tanqih

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the second definition of fiqh and explain what each component excludes from the scope of fiqh.

Core Idea
Fiqh is knowledge of practical legal rulings from detailed evidence – each term excludes a class of non-fiqh knowledge.

This lesson unpacks the famous second definition of fiqh from al-Tanqih. We will walk through each word in the definition to see what it adds and what it excludes. By understanding these exclusions, you will grasp the precise scope of fiqh as a discipline.

Guided Path
1
Identify the genus and differentiae
The definition is 'al-ilm bi al-ahkam al-shar'iyyah al-amaliyyah min adillatiha al-tafshiliyyah'. 'Al-ilm' is the genus covering all sciences; the rest are successive differentiae that narrow it down.
Each differentia removes a category of knowledge that is not fiqh.
2
Analyze the first differentia: 'al-ahkam'
If 'hukm' is taken in its customary meaning (ascription of one thing to another), then 'al-ahkam' excludes knowledge of mere concepts (tasawwurat), which lack ascription. Only affirmations (tasdiqat) remain.
This assumes the customary meaning of hukm; another meaning will be considered in the next lesson.
3
Analyze the second differentia: 'al-shar'iyyah'
This excludes rational rulings (e.g., 'the world is created') and sensory rulings (e.g., 'fire is burning'). It also excludes rulings that are from Allah but do not depend on the Law (e.g., obligation of faith). Such rulings are not considered 'shar'i' because they are not dependent on the Law.
The distinction between shar'i and ghayr shar'i is crucial in Hanafi usul.
4
Analyze the third differentia: 'al-amaliyyah'
This excludes theoretical rulings related to belief, like the ruling that ijma' is a source of law. Such rulings are not practical and hence not fiqh.
Fiqh deals with what a person does, not what they believe about the sources.
5
Analyze the phrase 'min adillatiha al-tafshiliyyah'
This specifies that fiqh knowledge must be derived from specific, detailed evidences (Quran, Sunnah, etc.), not from general principles or mere taqlid.
This distinguishes fiqh from usul al-fiqh, which deals with general evidence rules.

Source Text

متن تنقيح مع التوضيح وقيل العلم بالاحكام الشرعية العملية من ادلتها التفصيلية فالعلم جنس والباقي فصل ! ماتن تنقیح فقہ کی دوسری تعریف بیان کر رہا ہے جس کا حاصل یہ ہے کہ فقہ ان احکام کے جاننے کا نام ہے جو شرعیہ ہوں عملیہ ہوں اور یہ جاننا ادلہ تفصیلیہ سے ہو پھر مصنف فوائد قیود بیان کر رہے ہیں العلم درجہ جنس ہے تمام علوم کو شامل ہے اور باقی فصل میں پہلا فصل الاحکام ہے احکام حکم کی جمع ہے حکم کے دو معنی ہیں (۱) معنی عرفی ، وہ ہے استاد امر الى امر آخر (۲) حکم اصطلاحی ! وہ ہے خطاب الله تعالى المتعلق بافعال المكلفين الخ اگر اول معنی مراد ہو تو حکم کی قید سے ذات وصفات کا علم خارج ہو جائے گا یعنی تصورات ( تصور موضوع تصور محمول ) کا علم خارج ہو جائے گا کیونکہ تصورات میں اسناد امرالی امر آخر نہیں ہوتا صرف تصدیقات باقی رہیں گی اور الشرعیہ کی قید سے احکام عقلیہ اور احکام حسیہ کا علم خارج ہو جائے گا احکام عقلیہ کی مثال العالم حادث اور احکام حسیہ کی مثال النار محرقہ ، اگر حکم سے مراد خطاب اللہ ہو تو الا حکام کی قید سے ان احکام سے احتراز ہوگا جو خطاب اللہ کے علاوہ ہیں۔ فاتحکم بهذا النفسیر اس صورت میں الشرعیہ کی قید کو معتبر بنانے کیلئے حکم کی دو قسمیں بنانی ہوں گی (۱) حکم شرعی (۲) حکم غیر شرعی حکم شرعی کی تعریف خطاب اللہ بما يتوقف على الشرع یعنی اللہ تعالی کا خطاب اس چیز کے ساتھ جس چیز کا ثبوت موقوف علی الشرع ہو یا بعنوان دیگر حکم شرعی وہ ہے جس پر خطاب اللہ بھی ہو اور وہ شریعت پر بھی موقوف ہے مثلا وجوب صلوۃ وجوب زکوۃ وغیرہ (۲) حکم غیر شرعی کی تعریف یہ کی مالا توقف علی الشرع یعنی خطاب اللہ اس چیز کے ساتھ جو شریعت پر موقوف نہ ہو یا بعنوان دیگر حکم غیر شرعی وہ ہے جو خطاب اللہ تو ہولیکن شریعت پر موقوف نہ ہو مثلا وجوب ایمان وجوب تصدیق النبی وغیرہ یہ سب احکام خطاب اللہ ہیں لیکن شرعیہ نہیں کیونکہ شریعت پر موقوف نہیں بلکہ خود شریعت جو فرمان خدا فرمان مصطفیٰ کا نام ہے ان پر موقوف ہے اگر یہ دوبارہ شریعت پر موقوف ہو جائیں تو دور لازم آئے گا جب احکام کی دو قسمیں ہو گئیں تو الشرعیہ کی قید لگا کر غیر شرعی ( وجوب ایمان و تصدیق النبی ) کو خارج کر دیا کیونکہ ان کے علم کو فقہ نہیں کہا جاتا ثم الشرعی اما نظر پھر حکم شرعی بمعنی ما توقف على الشرع کی دو قسمیں ہیں (۱) عملی یعنی وہ حکم جو عمل سے متعلق ہو اور اس پر عمل کرنا مقصود ہو (۲) حکم شرعی نظری جو عقیدہ سے متعلق ہو اس پر عقیدہ رکھنا مقصود ہو جیسے الا جماع
Text of al-Tanqih with al-Tawdih: 'And it was said: knowledge of the practical legal rulings from their detailed evidences.' So 'knowledge' is the genus, and the rest are differentiae. The author of al-Tanqih is explaining the second definition of fiqh. Its gist is that fiqh is the name for knowing those rulings which are legal and practical, and this knowing is from detailed evidences. Then the author discusses the benefits of the restrictions. 'Knowledge' is the genus, including all sciences, and the rest are differentiae. The first differentia is 'rulings'. 'Rulings' is the plural of 'ruling'. 'Ruling' has two meanings: (1) the customary meaning: it is the ascription of one thing to another; (2) the terminological meaning: it is the address of Allah Almighty related to the actions of the obligated persons, etc. If the first meaning is intended, then the restriction 'rulings' excludes knowledge of essence and attributes, i.e., knowledge of concepts (concept of subject, concept of predicate) because in concepts there is no ascription of one thing to another; only affirmations remain. And the restriction 'legal' excludes rational rulings and sensory rulings. An example of rational rulings: 'the world is created'; an example of sensory rulings: 'fire is burning'. If by 'ruling' is meant the address of Allah, then the restriction 'rulings' avoids those rulings that are other than the address of Allah. According to this interpretation, to make the restriction 'legal' valid, two divisions of rulings must be made: (1) legal ruling (shar'i); (2) non-legal ruling. The definition of legal ruling: the address of Allah concerning that which depends on the Law, i.e., the address of Allah Almighty concerning a thing whose existence depends on the Law. Or, by another expression, a legal ruling is that which is both the address of Allah and depends on the Law, like the obligation of prayer, the obligation of zakat, etc. (2) The definition of non-legal ruling: that which does not depend on the Law, i.e., the address of Allah concerning a thing that is not dependent on the Law. Or, by another expression, a non-legal ruling is that which is the address of Allah but does not depend on the Law, like the obligation of faith, the obligation of affirming the Prophet, etc. All these are rulings from the address of Allah but are not legal because they do not depend on the Law; rather the Law itself (which is the command of God and the command of Mustafa) depends on them. If they themselves depended on the Law, then a vicious circle would occur. When two divisions of rulings exist, the restriction 'legal' excludes non-legal rulings (obligation of faith and affirmation of the Prophet) because knowledge of them is not called fiqh. Then the legal ruling, in the sense of that which depends on the Law, is of two types: (1) practical: a ruling related to action, and acting upon it is intended; (2) theoretical legal ruling: related to belief, and holding that belief is intended, like consensus (ijma').
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the second definition of fiqh as presented in al-Tanqih and explained by al-Tawdih. The definition is: 'Knowledge of the practical legal rulings from their detailed evidences.' The author breaks down each term. 'al-Ilm' (knowledge) is the genus covering all sciences. 'al-Ahkam' (rulings) is the first differentia, excluding knowledge of concepts (tasawwurat) like 'the world is created' or 'fire is burning'—these are rational or sensory judgments, not legal rulings. If 'hukm' is taken in the terminological sense (address of Allah), then 'shar'iyyah' (legal) restricts it to rulings that depend on the Law (like prayer and zakat), excluding rulings that are from Allah but do not depend on the Law, such as the obligation to believe (iman) and affirm the Prophet (tasdiq). Those are called non-legal rulings; they are a priori and do not themselves depend on the Law, otherwise a circularity would arise. Finally, 'amaliyyah' (practical) excludes theoretical rulings concerning beliefs, like the consensus of scholars (ijma') which is a basis for law but not a practical ruling itself. Thus, fiqh is confined to rulings that are practical, legal, and derived from detailed evidence.

Key Terms
حکم شرعی (ḥukm shar‘ī): The address of Allah concerning an action that depends on the Law for its existence, e.g., obligation of prayer.
دلیل تفصیلی (dalīl tafṣīlī): Specific evidence for a particular ruling, as opposed to the general principles of usul al-fiqh.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is excluded from fiqh by the term 'al-amaliyyah'?
A) Knowledge of the obligation of prayer.
B) Knowledge of the obligation of zakat.
C) Knowledge that ijma' is a source of law.
D) Knowledge that the world is created.

Key Takeaway
Fiqh is knowledge of practical legal rulings derived from detailed evidence, excluding conceptual, rational, sensory, non-legal, and theoretical knowledge.

The Definition of Fiqh and Its Qayood
Parsing the definition of fiqh with each qayd

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify each qayd in the definition of fiqh and explain which types of knowledge it excludes, following the Hanafi usul al-fiqh tradition.

Core Idea
Fiqh is knowledge of shar'i practical rulings from detailed evidences—each qualifier excludes a specific non-fiqh knowledge type.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, fiqh is defined with precision. This passage from Sharh al-Tawdih and its commentary dissects the definition 'knowledge of the shar'i practical rulings from their detailed evidences.' Each qualifier (qayd) serves to exclude a type of knowledge that might be mistaken for fiqh. Understanding these exclusions is key to grasping the scope of ijtihad and the role of the mujtahid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the genus and differentia
The definition has five components: 'knowledge' is the genus; 'shar'i', 'practical', 'from detailed evidences' are the differentiae. Recognize that the first qayd is 'al-ahkam' (the rulings) which is part of the differential.
Some may miss that 'al-ahkam' is the first qualifier after 'knowledge'.
2
Understand the two meanings of hukm
Hukm has two possible meanings: conventional (attribution of one thing to another) and terminological (address of Allah). Under each, the exclusions differ. Analyze both scenarios as presented in the text.
The author of al-Tawdih entertains both possibilities.
3
Process the qayd 'shar'iyya'
This qualifier excludes rational rulings (e.g., 'the world is originated'), sensory rulings (e.g., 'fire burns'), and positive rulings (e.g., 'the subject is nominative'). Also, under the terminological meaning, it separates shar'i from non-shar'i, excluding obligation of faith etc.
Non-shar'i rulings are Allah's address but not dependent on shari'ah.
4
Process the qayd 'amaliyya'
This excludes theoretical/creedal shar'i rulings such as 'consensus is a proof' or 'faith is obligatory'. Only rulings related to action (practical) are fiqh.
Creedal matters belong to usul al-din, not fiqh.
5
Process the qayd 'min adillatiha al-tafsiliyya'
This excludes: (a) knowledge of the muqallid who follows a mujtahid without directly deriving from the four sources; (b) knowledge obtained from general principles (muqtadi/nafi) without detailed evidence. Also note the refutation of Ibn al-Hajib's addition of 'bil-istidlal' as redundant.
Ibn al-Hajib's addition is considered unnecessary because the detailed evidences already imply istidlal.

Source Text

وقيل العلم بالاحكام الشرعية العملية من ادلتها التفصيلية فالعلم جنس والباقي فصل ! ماتن تنفقیح فقہ کی دوسری تعریف بیان کر رہا ہے جس کا حاصل یہ ہے کہ فقہ ان احکام کے جاننے کا نام ہے جو شرعیہ ہوں عملیہ ہوں اور یہ جاننا ادلہ تفصیلیہ سے ہو پھر مصنف فوائد قیود بیان کر رہے ہیں العلم درجہ جنس ہے تمام علوم کو شامل ہے اور باقی فصل میں پہلا فصل الاحکام ہے احکام حکم کی جمع ہے حکم کے دو معنی ہیں (۱) معنی عرفی ، وہ ہے استاد امر الى امر آخر (۲) حکم اصطلاحی ! وہ ہے خطاب الله تعالى المتعلق بافعال المكلفين الخ اگر اول معنی مراد ہو تو حکم کی قید سے ذات وصفات کا علم خارج ہو جائے گا یعنی تصورات ( تصور موضوع تصور محمول ) کا علم خارج ہو جائے گا کیونکہ تصورات میں اسناد امرالی امر آخر نہیں ہوتا صرف تصدیقات باقی رہیں گی اور الشرعیہ کی قید سے احکام عقلیہ اور احکام حسیہ کا علم خارج ہو جائے گا احکام عقلیہ کی مثال العالم حادث اور احکام حسیہ کی مثال النار محرقہ ، اگر حکم سے مراد خطاب اللہ ہو تو الا حکام کی قید سے ان احکام سے احتراز ہوگا جو خطاب اللہ کے علاوہ ہیں۔ فاتحکم بهذا النفسیر اس صورت میں الشرعیہ کی قید کو معتبر بنانے کیلئے حکم کی دو قسمیں بنانی ہوں گی (۱) حکم شرعی (۲) حکم غیر شرعی حکم شرعی کی تعریف خطاب اللہ بما يتوقف على الشرع یعنی اللہ تعالی کا خطاب اس چیز کے ساتھ جس چیز کا ثبوت موقوف علی الشرع ہو یا بعنوان دیگر حکم شرعی وہ ہے جس پر خطاب اللہ بھی ہو اور وہ شریعت پر بھی موقوف ہے مثلا وجوب صلوۃ وجوب زکوۃ وغیرہ (۲) حکم غیر شرعی کی تعریف یہ کی مالا توقف علی الشرع یعنی خطاب اللہ اس چیز کے ساتھ جو شریعت پر موقوف نہ ہو یا بعنوان دیگر حکم غیر شرعی وہ ہے جو خطاب اللہ تو ہولیکن شریعت پر موقوف نہ ہو مثلا وجوب ایمان وجوب تصدیق النبی وغیرہ یہ سب احکام خطاب اللہ ہیں لیکن شرعیہ نہیں کیونکہ شریعت پر موقوف نہیں بلکہ خود شریعت جو فرمان خدا فرمان مصطفیٰ کا نام ہے ان پر موقوف ہے اگر یہ دوبارہ شریعت پر موقوف ہو جائیں تو دور لازم آئے گا جب احکام کی دو قسمیں ہو گئیں تو الشرعیہ کی قید لگا کر غیر شرعی ( وجوب ایمان و تصدیق النبی ) کو خارج کر دیا کیونکہ ان کے علم کو فقہ نہیں کہا جاتا ثم الشرعی اما نظر پھر حکم شرعی بمعنی ما توقف على الشرع کی دو قسمیں ہیں (۱) عملی یعنی وہ حکم جو عمل سے متعلق ہو اور اس پر عمل کرنا مقصود ہو (۲) حکم شرعی نظری جو عقیدہ سے متعلق ہو اس پر عقیدہ رکھنا مقصود ہو جیسے الا جماع حجتہ یہ حکم نظری ہے اس کا علم فقہ نہیں کہلائے گا تو العملیہ کی قید لگا کر احکام شرعی نظری کو خارج کر دیا قوله من ادلتها التفصيلیہ اس کی وضاحت کرتے ہوئے مصنف فرماتے ہیں کہ من اول تھا جار مجرور ظرف مستقر الحاصل کے متعلق ہو کر العلم کی صفت ہے مقصد یہ ہوگا کہ احکام شرعیہ عملیہ کا علم حاصل ہونے والا ہو ( اس شخص موصوف بالعلم کو ) ادلہ سے اور ادلہ سے مخصوص ادلہ ( کتاب سنت اجماع قیاس) مراد ہیں لہذا من ادل تھا سے علم مقلد خارج ہو جائے گا کیونکہ اس کا علم ادلر مخصوصہ اربعہ سے حاصل نہیں ہوتا بلکہ اس کی دلیل صرف قول مجتہد و قول مفتی ہوتا ہے، وقولہ اتفصیلیۃ یہ بھی قید احترازی ہے اس سے وہ علم خارج ہو جائے گا جو ادلہ اجمالیہ سے حاصل ہو ادلہ اجمالیہ سے مراد مقتضی اور نافی ہیں مثلا یوں کہا جائے ہذا حکم ثابت دلیل لوجود مقتضی اور ہذا الحکم لیس بثابت لوجود النافی، اس جیسے حکم کو فقہ نہیں کہا جائے گا۔ وقد زاد، ابن الحاجب مقصود مصنف رد علی ابن الحاجب علامہ ابن الحاجب نے اس تعریف میں بالاستدلال کی قید کا اضافہ کیا ہے مصنف اس پر اشکال کر رہے ہیں کہ یہ قید مکرر ہے کیونکہ پہلے من اول تھاذ کر کر دیا ہے تو جو علم ادلہ سے حاصل ہوگا وہ استدلال کے ساتھ ہی ہوگا لہذا اس کے بعد دوبارہ بالاستدلال کا ذکر تکرار محض ہے، نیز بالاستدلال کی قید سے مقصود ابن الحاجب علم مقلد کو خارج کرنا ہے اور وہ پہلے ہی من اول تھا اتفصیلیہ سے خارج ہو چکا ہے لہذا اس کا ذکر بلاشک تکرار ہے۔
شرح تلویح
قوله وقيل العلم عرف اصحاب الشافعی غرض شارح توضیح متن ! سب سے پہلے یہ بتلایا کہ تعریف ثانى هو العلم بالاحكام الخ امام شافعی کے اصحاب نے کی ہے اس کے بعد اس کی تعریف کو دبیان ذالک سے وجہ حصر کے طریقہ پر اس طرح بیان کیا کہ قیودات احتراز یہ کا فائدہ واضح ہو جائے چنانچہ فرماتے ہیں کہ علم کا متعلق (جس چیز سے علم تعلق پکڑ رہا ہے ) یا حکم ہوگا یا غیر حکم اگر غیر حکم ہے تو اسے فقہ نہیں کہا جائے گا۔ لہذا بالا حکام کی قید سے ذات وصفات ( تصورات ) کا علم خارج من الفقہ ہو جائے گا اگر متعلق علم حکم ہے تو دو حال سے خالی نہیں یا وہ حکم شرع سے ماخوذ ہوگا یا نہیں اگر شرع سے ماخوذ نہیں ہے تو اس کو فقہ نہیں کہا جائے گا مثلاً وہ حکم جو عقل سے ماخوذ ہو جن کو احکام عقلیہ کہا جاتا ہے جیسے العالم حادث یا وہ حکم جو حس سے ماخوذ وثابت ہو مثلا النار محرفه یا وہ حکم جو وضع و اصطلاح سے ماخوذ ہو جن کو احکام وضعیہ کہا جاتا ہے مثلا الفاعل مرفوع یہ تمام احکام عقلیه و حسیہ وضعیہ (الشرعیہ کی قید کی وجہ سے ) فقہ سے خارج ہو جائیں گے اگر وہ حکم ماخوذ من الشرع ہے تو پھر اس کی دو صورتیں ہیں کیفیت عمل کے ساتھ تعلق ہوگا یا نہ اگر کیفیت عمل کے ساتھ متعلق نہیں ہے تو ان کو بھی فقہ نہیں کہا جائے گا جیسے احکام نظر یہ شرعیہ جن کو احکام اعتقادیہ واصلیہ بھی کہا جاتا ہے مثلا اجتماع کا حجت ہونا اور کون الایمان و اجبا و غیرہ تو العملیہ کی قید سے احکام نظریہ خارج ہو جاتے ہیں
Text of Tanqih with al-Tawdih: And it is said: knowledge of the shar'i practical rulings from their detailed evidences. So 'knowledge' is the genus and the rest is differentia. The author of al-Tanqih is presenting the second definition of fiqh, the gist of which is that fiqh is the name for knowing those rulings which are shar'i and practical, and this knowing is from detailed evidences. Then the author mentions the benefits of the qayood. 'Knowledge' is a genus including all sciences, and the rest is differentia. The first differentia is 'rulings' (al-ahkam). 'Ahkam' is plural of 'hukm'. Hukm has two meanings: (1) conventional meaning: the attribution of one thing to another; (2) terminological meaning: the address of Allah Most High related to the actions of the obligated individuals. If the first meaning is intended, then the qayd of 'hukm' excludes knowledge of essence and attributes, i.e., conceptual knowledge, because in conceptual knowledge there is no attribution of one thing to another; only affirmations remain. And by the qayd of 'al-shar'iyya', rational rulings and sensory rulings are excluded. Example of rational rulings: 'the world is originated'; example of sensory rulings: 'fire burns'. If by 'hukm' is meant the address of Allah, then by the qayd of 'al-ahkam' they avoid rulings other than the address of Allah. According to this interpretation, to make the qayd of 'al-shar'iyya' valid, two divisions of hukm must be made: (1) shar'i hukm, (2) non-shar'i hukm. Definition of shar'i hukm: the address of Allah concerning something the proof of which depends on shari'ah, i.e., the address of Allah about a thing whose establishment depends on shari'ah; or another expression: shar'i hukm is that which is both the address of Allah and depends on shari'ah, e.g., obligation of prayer, obligation of zakat, etc. (2) Definition of non-shar'i hukm: it is not dependent on shari'ah, i.e., the address of Allah concerning something which does not depend on shari'ah; or another expression: non-shar'i hukm is that which is the address of Allah but does not depend on shari'ah, e.g., obligation of faith, obligation of affirming the Prophet, etc. All these are addresses of Allah but not shar'i because they do not depend on shari'ah; rather shari'ah itself (which is the commands of God and the Prophet) depends on them. If they were again dependent on shari'ah, there would be a vicious circle. When there are two divisions of ahkam, by adding the qayd of 'al-shar'iyya', non-shar'i (obligation of faith and affirming the Prophet) are excluded because their knowledge is not called fiqh. Then, al-shar'i (with the meaning of what depends on shari'ah) is of two types: (1) 'amali (practical) — a ruling related to action where performing the action is intended; (2) nazari (theoretical) — related to belief where holding the belief is intended, such as consensus being a proof. This is a theoretical ruling, its knowledge is not called fiqh. So by the qayd of 'al-'amaliyya', theoretical shar'i rulings are excluded. His statement: 'from its detailed evidences' — the author explains that 'min' is preparatory, the prepositional phrase is related to an implicit hal (state) and is a description of 'knowledge'. The purpose is that knowledge of shar'i practical rulings is obtained (by the person described with knowledge) from evidences, and by evidences are meant the specific evidences (Quran, Sunnah, consensus, analogy). Hence, by 'min adillatiha' the knowledge of the muqallid is excluded because his knowledge is not obtained from the four specific evidences; rather his evidence is only the statement of the mujtahid or mufti. And his statement: 'al-tafsiliyya' — this is also a qualifying qayd; by it, knowledge obtained from non-detailed evidences is excluded. By non-detailed evidences are meant the muqtadi (requiring cause) and nafi (preventing cause), e.g., saying 'this ruling is established due to the existence of the muqtadi' or 'this ruling is not established due to the existence of the nafi'. Such knowledge is not called fiqh. And Ibn al-Hajib added the qayd of 'bil-istidlal'. The author's purpose is to refute Ibn al-Hajib: Allamah Ibn al-Hajib added the qayd of 'bil-istidlal' in this definition. The author objects that this qayd is redundant because earlier 'from its evidences' was mentioned, so the knowledge obtained from evidences will necessarily be with istidlal. Therefore, mentioning 'bil-istidlal' again is pure repetition. Moreover, the purpose of Ibn al-Hajib in adding 'bil-istidlal' is to exclude the knowledge of the muqallid, and that has already been excluded by the previous 'min adillatiha al-tafsiliyya'. Hence, its mention is undoubtedly repetition.
Sharh al-Talwih: His statement: 'And it is said: knowledge...' was defined by the followers of Imam al-Shafi'i. The purpose of the commentator of al-Tawdih (the text) is to first explain that the second definition is the one made by the followers of Imam al-Shafi'i. Then he explains this definition in the manner of 'bayyan dhalika' in a comprehensive way so that the benefit of the qualifying qayood becomes clear. He says: the object of knowledge is either hukm or non-hukm. If it is non-hukm, it is not called fiqh. Hence, by the qayd of 'al-ahkam', knowledge of essence and attributes (concepts) is excluded from fiqh. If the object of knowledge is hukm, then either it is derived from shari'ah or not. If not derived from shari'ah, it is not called fiqh — e.g., rulings derived from reason (rational rulings), like 'the world is originated', or from sense (sensory rulings) like 'fire burns', or from convention (positive rulings) like 'the subject is in the nominative case'. All these rational, sensory, and positive rulings are excluded from fiqh due to the qayd of 'al-shar'iyya'. If the ruling is derived from shari'ah, then it is either related to the quality of action or not. If not related to action, it is also not called fiqh, like theoretical shar'i rulings (also called creedal rulings) e.g., that consensus is a proof, and that faith is obligatory, etc. So by the qayd of 'al-'amaliyya', theoretical rulings are excluded.
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the definition of fiqh as 'knowledge of the shar'i practical rulings from their detailed evidences.' The author, Sadr al-Shari'ah in al-Tawdih, and then the commentator (likely the author of Misk al-Malih) explain each qayd (qualifying term) and its purpose. The definition is attributed to the followers of Imam al-Shafi'i. The commentator parses the definition: 'knowledge' is the genus; the rest ('shar'i', 'practical', 'from detailed evidences') are differentia. Two meanings of 'hukm' are considered: the conventional (attribution) and the terminological (address of Allah). Under each meaning, the qayood exclude irrelevant types of knowledge. For example, if hukm means attribution, then conceptual knowledge (essence and attributes) is excluded. If it means address of Allah, then rulings not from Allah are excluded, and within Allah's address, a distinction is made between shar'i (dependent on revelation) and non-shar'i (like obligation of faith, which is rational and not dependent on revelation). Further, among shar'i rulings, practical ones are included, while theoretical (creedal) ones are excluded. The qayd 'min adillatiha al-tafsiliyya' ensures that only knowledge derived from the four sources (Quran, Sunnah, Ijma, Qiyas) by ijtihad is fiqh, excluding the muqallid's knowledge (based on a mujtahid's statement) and knowledge from general principles (like muqtadi and nafi). The commentator also refutes Ibn al-Hajib's addition of 'bil-istidlal' as redundant. The Sharh al-Talwih section by Taftazani then re-explains the same definition in a structured manner, confirming the exclusions. This lesson is a typical usul al-fiqh parsing exercise, clarifying the technical boundaries of the term 'fiqh'.

Key Terms
حکم (hukm): Two meanings: conventional (attribution of one thing to another) and terminological (address of Allah related to the actions of obligated individuals).
شرعیہ (shar'iyya): Pertaining to shari'ah; rulings that depend on revelation for their establishment.
عملیہ (amaliyya): Practical, relating to action, as opposed to theoretical/creedal.
ادلتہ تفصیلیہ (adillatuhu al-tafsiliyya): The detailed evidences: Quran, Sunnah, Ijma, and Qiyas, applied to a specific case.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the definition explained in this passage, why is the knowledge of a muqallid (one who blindly follows) not considered fiqh?
A) Because a muqallid's knowledge is not derived from detailed evidences (adillah tafsiliyya).
B) Because a muqallid does not have knowledge of practical rulings.
C) Because a muqallid does not believe in the four sources.
D) Because a muqallid's knowledge is based on sensory evidence.

Key Takeaway
Fiqh is strictly defined as ijtihad-derived knowledge of practical shari'ah rulings, excluding every other type of knowledge.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
intermediate
8 min
The Hanafi Definition of Fiqh and Its Qualifiers
Explaining the Hanafi definition of fiqh and the role of each qualifier

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of fiqh, explain the role of each qualifier, and evaluate the redundancy of the qualifier 'bi al-istidlal'.

Core Idea
Fiqh is knowledge of practical shari'i rulings derived from detailed evidences—each qualifier excludes a specific non-fiqh knowledge.

This lesson unpacks the classical Hanafi definition of fiqh: 'al-ilm bi al-ahkam al-shar'iyyah al-amaliyyah min adillatiha al-tafsiliyyah'. We will examine each qualifier to see what it excludes, and then discuss a scholarly dispute about whether an additional qualifier 'bi al-istidlal' is necessary.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Definition and Its Components
Write out the full definition: 'al-ilm bi al-ahkam al-shar'iyyah al-amaliyyah min adillatiha al-tafsiliyyah'. Break it into its five key elements: 'ilm, ahkam, shar'iyyah, amaliyyah, min adillatiha al-tafsiliyyah.
2
Analyze Each Qualifier's Excluding Function
For each element, determine what type of knowledge it excludes: 'ahkam' excludes essences; 'shar'iyyah' excludes rational and conventional rulings; 'amaliyyah' excludes theoretical beliefs; 'min adillatiha' excludes taqlid; 'tafsiliyyah' excludes knowledge from general (ijmali) evidences.
Pay special attention to the difference between detailed (tafsili) and general (ijmali) evidences.
3
Differentiate Fiqh from Taqlid
Understand why the knowledge of a muqallid (one who blindly follows) is not considered fiqh: it is not derived from the four primary sources through ijtihad. The muqallid's evidence is merely the statement of a mujtahid.
The qualifier 'min adillatiha' alone suffices to exclude taqlid.
4
Examine the Dispute with Ibn al-Hajib
Ibn al-Hajib added 'bi al-istidlal' to the definition. The Hanafi commentator argues this is redundant because 'min adillatiha' already implies istidlal (deduction). Consider the opposing view: Ibn al-Hajib wanted to explicitly exclude taqlid.
The Hanafi response: taqlid is already excluded by 'min adillatiha al-tafsiliyyah', so the addition is unnecessary.
5
Apply the Definition to Examples
Test the definition: Is knowledge of a ruling from a reliable fatwa considered fiqh? Is knowledge that prayer is obligatory based on a Quranic verse considered fiqh? Use the qualifiers to classify each.
Only knowledge derived directly from detailed evidences through ijtihad qualifies as fiqh.

مسک الملیح) حجتہ یہ حکم نظری ہے اس کا علم فقہ نہیں کہلائے گا تو العملیہ کی قید لگا کر احکام شرعی نظری کو خارج کر دیا و قولہ من ادلتها التفصيليہ اس کی وضاحت کرتے ہوئے مصنف فرماتے ہیں کہ من الادلہ جار مجرور ظرف مستقر الحاصل کے متعلق ہو کر العلم کی صفت ہے مقصد یہ ہوگا کہ احکام شرعیہ عملیہ کا علم حاصل ہونے والا ہو ( اس شخص موصوف بالعلم کو ) ادلہ سے اور ادلہ سے مخصوص ادلہ ( کتاب سنت اجماع قیاس) مراد ہیں لہذا من الادلہ سے علم مقلد خارج ہو جائے گا کیونکہ اس کا علم ادلہ مخصوصہ اربعہ سے حاصل نہیں ہوتا بلکہ اس کی دلیل صرف قول مجتہد و قول مفتی ہوتا ہے، وقولہ اتفصيليۃ یہ بھی قید احترازی ہے اس سے وہ علم خارج ہو جائے گا جو ادلہ اجمالیہ سے حاصل ہو ادلہ اجمالیہ سے مراد مقتضی اور نافی ہیں مثلا یوں کہا جائے ہذا حکم ثابت دلیل لوجود مقتضی اور ہذا الحکم لیس بثابت لوجود النافی، اس جیسے حکم کو فقہ نہیں کہا جائے گا۔ وقد زاد ابن الحاجب مقصود مصنف رد علی ابن الحاجب علامہ ابن الحاجب نے اس تعریف میں بالاستدلال کی قید کا اضافہ کیا ہے مصنف اس پر اشکال کر رہے ہیں کہ یہ قید مکرر ہے کیونکہ پہلے من الادلہ ذکر کر دیا ہے تو جو علم ادلہ سے حاصل ہوگا وہ استدلال کے ساتھ ہی ہوگا لہذا اس کے بعد دوبارہ بالاستدلال کا ذکر تکرار محض ہے، نیز بالاستدلال کی قید سے مقصود ابن الحاجب علم مقلد کو خارج کرنا ہے اور وہ پہلے ہی من الادلہ التفصیلیہ سے خارج ہو چکا ہے لہذا اس کا ذکر بلاشک تکرار ہے۔
From Misk al-Malih: The proof: This ruling is theoretical, so its knowledge would not be called fiqh. Thus, the qualifier 'al-'amaliyyah' excludes theoretical shar'i rulings. And his saying 'min adillatiha al-tafṣiliyyah' – the author explains that 'min al-adillah' is a jar-majrur related to a hidden state, and it is an adjective of 'al-'ilm'. The intent is that knowledge of practical shar'i rulings is obtained (from the person characterized by knowledge) from the evidences, and the evidences are specifically Kitab, Sunnah, Ijma', and Qiyas. So by 'min al-adillah', the knowledge of a muqallid is excluded because his knowledge is not obtained from the specific four evidences; rather, his evidence is only the saying of a mujtahid or mufti. And his saying 'al-tafṣiliyyah' is also a restrictive qualifier; it excludes knowledge that is obtained from general (ijmali) evidences. General evidences refer to the inferred cause (muqtadi) and the preventer (nafi), e.g. saying 'this ruling is established due to the existence of the inferred cause' and 'this ruling is not established due to the existence of the preventer'. Such a ruling is not called fiqh. Ibn al-Hajib added the qualifier 'bi al-istidlal' in this definition. The author criticizes this, saying that this qualifier is redundant because he already mentioned 'min al-adillah', so knowledge obtained from evidence necessarily involves istidlal. Hence, mentioning 'bi al-istidlal' again is pure repetition. Moreover, Ibn al-Hajib intended to exclude the muqallid's knowledge by this qualifier, but that is already excluded by 'min al-adillah al-tafṣiliyyah', so its mention is undoubtedly repetition.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage is a Hanafi usul commentary explaining the definition of fiqh. The author carefully unpacks each word of the definition: 'al-ilm bi al-ahkam al-shar'iyyah al-amaliyyah min adillatiha al-tafṣiliyyah'. He shows how each qualifier serves to exclude other types of knowledge. For example, 'al-ahkam' excludes knowledge of essences and attributes; 'al-shar'iyyah' excludes rational, sensory, and conventional rulings; 'al-amaliyyah' excludes theoretical beliefs; and 'min adillatiha al-tafṣiliyyah' excludes knowledge that is not derived from the four sources through ijtihad, such as taqlid or divine knowledge. The commentator then addresses Ibn al-Hajib's addition of 'bi al-istidlal', arguing it is redundant because 'min al-adillah' already implies istidlal. This critique underscores the Hanafi commitment to precise, non-redundant definitions. The discussion is purely analytical and does not introduce any new fatwa.

Key Terms
الفقه (al-fiqh): Knowledge of practical shar'i rulings derived from detailed evidences
الادلۃ التفصیلیۃ (al-adillah al-tafṣiliyyah): The specific evidences: Qur'an, Sunnah, Ijma', and Qiyas
المقلد (al-muqallid): One who follows a mujtahid without knowledge of the evidences
الاستدلال (al-istidlal): The process of deducing a ruling from evidence
الادلۃ الاجمالیۃ (al-adillah al-ijmaliyyah): General evidences referring to inferred causes (muqtadi) and preventers (nafi), without specific textual citation

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi definition, which of the following is considered fiqh?
A) A muqallid's knowledge that prayer is obligatory.
B) Knowledge that prayer is obligatory derived from the Quranic verse 'Aqim al-salat' and its usul interpretation.
C) Knowledge that the universe is created from rational arguments.
D) Knowledge that wine is prohibited based on a sensory experience.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definition of fiqh is precise: it requires knowledge of practical shar'i rulings derived directly from detailed evidences, excluding taqlid and general reasoning.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
intermediate

Shafi'i Definition of Fiqh and Its Qualifiers
Analyzing the Shafi'i definition of fiqh using wajh hasr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Shafi'i definition of fiqh and explain how each qualifier excludes certain types of knowledge.

Core Idea
The four qualifiers of the Shafi'i definition systematically exclude all non-fiqh knowledge, leaving only ijtihad-derived practical shar'i rulings.

The Shafi'i school formulated a precise definition of fiqh using a method of comprehensive exclusion. Each term in 'knowledge of the practical shar'i rulings derived from their detailed evidences' serves a purpose, carving out the exact domain of fiqh. This lesson breaks down that definition to clarify what counts as fiqh and what does not.

Guided Path
1
Identify the full definition from the Shafi'i school
The definition is: العلم بالأحكام الشرعية العملية من أدلتها التفصيلية. This is the standard definition taught in usul.
Memorize this definition – it is foundational.
2
List the qualifiers
The four key qualifiers are: الأحكام (rulings), الشرعية (shar'i), العملية (practical), من أدلتها التفصيلية (from detailed evidences).
Each qualifier restricts the scope.
3
Determine what each qualifier excludes
Each qualifier acts as an احتراز (exclusion). 'الأحكام' excludes essences/attributes; 'الشرعية' excludes rational, sensory, and conventional rulings; 'العملية' excludes theoretical beliefs; 'من أدلتها التفصيلية' excludes knowledge not derived through ijtihad from the four sources.
The commentator illustrates exclusions with examples.
4
Consider special cases: knowledge of Allah, the Prophet, Jibril, and the muqallid
These are excluded because they are not obtained by ijtihad from detailed evidences. The muqallid's knowledge is based on the mujtahid's statement, not direct derivation. The Prophet's ijtihad is rare and after ijtihad it is supported by revelation, so the term fiqh does not apply.
This clarifies that fiqh is inherently tied to ijtihad.
5
Summarize the result of comprehensive restriction
The method of وجہ حصر shows that fiqh is exclusively the knowledge that meets all four conditions. The full definition is precise and leaves no room for ambiguity.
This method is a standard tool in defining terms in usul.

This passage explains the Shafi'i definition of fiqh using the method of comprehensive restriction. Each qualifier excludes certain categories: 'al-ahkam' excludes non-rulings; 'al-shar'iyyah' excludes non-shar'i rulings; 'al-'amaliyyah' excludes theoretical rulings; 'min adillatiha al-tafṣiliyyah' excludes knowledge not derived from detailed evidences, such as the knowledge of Allah, the Prophet, Jibril, and the muqallid. It also addresses the objection regarding the Prophet's ijtihad, stating it is rare and after ijtihad his rulings are supported by revelation, so the term fiqh does not apply. Finally, it discusses the term 'hukm' and its three meanings: customary, usuli (the address of Allah related to acts of the obligated), and logical.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the definition of fiqh according to the Shafi'i school, as explained in al-Tawdih. The commentator uses the method of comprehensive restriction (wajh hasr) to show how each qualifier excludes certain types of knowledge. For example, 'al-ahkam' excludes essences and attributes; 'al-shar'iyyah' excludes rational, sensory, and conventional rulings; 'al-'amaliyyah' excludes theoretical beliefs; and 'min adillatiha al-tafṣiliyyah' excludes knowledge that is not derived through ijtihad from the four sources, such as divine knowledge or taqlid. The commentator also addresses the objection that the Prophet's ijtihad would be included, and explains it is negligible. Then the passage transitions to a discussion of the term 'hukm' itself, distinguishing its three meanings: customary, usuli, and logical. The usuli definition of hukm as Allah's address related to acts of the obligated is central to our discipline. This detailed analysis helps the student understand the precise scope of fiqh.

Key Terms
الفقہ (al-fiqh): Knowledge of practical shar'i rulings derived from detailed evidences (Shafi'i definition).
وجہ الحصر (wajh al-hasr): A method of definition that uses restrictive qualifiers to exclude all other possibilities, leaving only the intended meaning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Knowledge of Allah
B) Knowledge of the muqallid
C) Knowledge of the Prophet
D) All of the above

Key Takeaway
The four qualifiers of the Shafi'i definition of fiqh work together to ensure that only ijtihad-derived practical shar'i rulings count as fiqh, excluding all other forms of religious knowledge.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling

The Three Meanings of 'Hukm' in Islamic Disciplines
Analyzing the multiple meanings of 'hukm' (customary, usuli, logical).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three meanings of the term 'hukm' and identify which meaning is intended in different contexts.

Core Idea
The term 'hukm' has at least three distinct meanings: customary, usuli, and logical; confusing them leads to misunderstanding in classical texts.

The term 'hukm' is central to usul al-fiqh, but it is used in multiple disciplines with different meanings. This passage clarifies that 'hukm' can refer to a judgment in everyday language (customary), the divine address regarding obligation and prohibition (usuli), or a proposition's truth value in logic (logical). Recognizing these distinctions is essential for accurate reading.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three meanings of 'hukm' as listed in the passage
The passage states: 1) Customary meaning (العرفي): attribution of one thing to another, positively or negatively. 2) Usuli meaning (اصطلاح الأصول والفقہ): the address of Allah related to the acts of the obligated by way of demand or choice. 3) Logical meaning (اصطلاح المنطق): the propositional judgment (true/false).
The commentator notes that the author of al-Tawdih only mentioned two, but three are needed.
2
Define each meaning in detail
Customary: any sentence where one thing is predicated of another, e.g., 'Zaid is standing'. Usuli: divine commands (like 'pray', 'fast') and prohibitions. Logical: the truth value of a statement, e.g., 'Zaid is standing' is true or false.
Each meaning belongs to a different science: logic, usul, everyday language.
3
Identify the context where each is used
In fiqh and usul, 'hukm' refers to the usuli meaning. In logic books, it is the logical meaning. In everyday speech, it is the customary meaning.
A single sentence like 'Zakat is obligatory' has all three aspects, but we focus on the relevant one for our discipline.
4
Apply the distinction to a sample phrase
Take the phrase 'الصلاة واجبة'. Customary: a predicate 'واجبة' is attached to 'الصلاة'. Usuli: it is a divine ruling that prayer is obligatory. Logical: the proposition 'prayer is obligatory' is true in Islam.
This example shows how the same phrase can be analyzed from different angles.

Source Text

اصطلاح المنطق سے تیسر ا معنی حکم بیان کیا یہ
This passage explains the Shafi'i definition of fiqh using the method of comprehensive restriction. Each qualifier excludes certain categories: 'al-ahkam' excludes non-rulings; 'al-shar'iyyah' excludes non-shar'i rulings; 'al-'amaliyyah' excludes theoretical rulings; 'min adillatiha al-tafṣiliyyah' excludes knowledge not derived from detailed evidences, such as the knowledge of Allah, the Prophet, Jibril, and the muqallid. It also addresses the objection regarding the Prophet's ijtihad, stating it is rare and after ijtihad his rulings are supported by revelation, so the term fiqh does not apply. Finally, it discusses the term 'hukm' and its three meanings: customary, usuli (the address of Allah related to acts of the obligated), and logical.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the definition of fiqh according to the Shafi'i school, as explained in al-Tawdih. The commentator uses the method of comprehensive restriction (wajh hasr) to show how each qualifier excludes certain types of knowledge. For example, 'al-ahkam' excludes essences and attributes; 'al-shar'iyyah' excludes rational, sensory, and conventional rulings; 'al-'amaliyyah' excludes theoretical beliefs; and 'min adillatiha al-tafṣiliyyah' excludes knowledge that is not derived through ijtihad from the four sources, such as divine knowledge or taqlid. The commentator also addresses the objection that the Prophet's ijtihad would be included, and explains it is negligible. Then the passage transitions to a discussion of the term 'hukm' itself, distinguishing its three meanings: customary, usuli, and logical. The usuli definition of hukm as Allah's address related to acts of the obligated is central to our discipline. This detailed analysis helps the student understand the precise scope of fiqh.

Key Terms
الحکم العرفي (al-hukm al-ʿurfī): The customary meaning: the attribution of one thing to another, either affirmative or negative.
الحکم الاصولي (al-hukm al-uṣūlī): The address of Allah related to the acts of the obligated by demand or choice.
الحکم المنطقي (al-hukm al-manṭiqī): The logical judgment: the proposition's truth value (true/false).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How many distinct meanings of the term 'hukm' are discussed in this passage?
A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) Five

Key Takeaway
The term 'hukm' has three distinct meanings: customary (judgment), usuli (divine address), and logical (propositional truth); recognizing which is intended is crucial for accurate understanding.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
intermediate
10 min
The Three Meanings of Hukm in the Definition of Fiqh
Analyzing the three meanings of 'hukm' to refine the definition of fiqh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three meanings of 'hukm' and explain why the customary meaning is intended in the Hanafi definition of fiqh.

Core Idea
The term 'hukm' in fiqh's definition must mean a complete proposition, not a divine address or logical judgment.

Usul al-fiqh begins with precise definitions. The definition of fiqh itself contains the term 'hukm', which can be interpreted in multiple ways. This lesson walks through the three possible meanings and shows how Hanafi usul scholars refined the definition to avoid redundancy and logical errors.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three meanings
List the three distinct senses of 'hukm' given by Taftazani: customary (nisba tamma), terminological (khitab Allah), and logical (idrak al-nisba).
Each meaning belongs to a different discipline: common usage, usul al-fiqh, and logic.
2
Reject the logical meaning
Understand why the logical meaning (hukm = tasdiq) is rejected: it makes the definition circular because fiqh would become 'knowledge of knowledge'.
Tasdiq is itself knowledge, so defining fiqh as knowledge of tasdiq is tautological.
3
Reject the terminological meaning
Understand why the terminological meaning (hukm = khitab Allah) is rejected: it makes the qualifiers 'shar'i' and 'amali' redundant, as a divine address is already shar'i and related to actions.
The definition already contains these qualifiers; if hukm already implies them, they become superfluous.
4
Select the customary meaning
Recognize that the only remaining valid meaning is the customary one: a complete relational predication (nisba tamma khabariyya), i.e., a proposition.
This meaning avoids redundancy and circularity.
5
Apply to the definition
Understand that the definition of fiqh becomes 'assent (tasdiq) to religious practical propositions derived from detailed proofs'.
The commentator Taftazani explicitly derives this result.
